# Comprehensive Scientific Exercise Database v2.0

**Sessions 1-15 COMPLETE: Batches 1a, 1b, 2a, 2b, 3a, 3b, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8 COMPLETE (355 exercises completed)**
- Sessions 1-5: CHEST, BACK, SHOULDERS, BICEPS, TRICEPS, QUADRICEPS, HAMSTRINGS/GLUTES, CORE (80 exercises)
- Session 6: CALVES, FOREARMS, NECK (30 exercises)
- Session 7 (Batch 1a): ABDOMINALS (Lower Abs, Obliques, Total Core) (20 exercises)
- Session 7 (Batch 1b): ABDOMINALS (Upper/Total Core) & BACK (20 exercises)
- Session 9 (Batch 2a): BACK ROWING EXERCISES (20 exercises)
- Session 9 (Batch 2b): BACK CABLE & BICEPS EXERCISES (20 exercises: 3 back + 17 biceps)
- Session 10 (Batch 3a): ADVANCED BICEPS VARIATIONS (20 exercises: lying cable, overhead cable, preacher, seated, single-arm)
- Session 10 (Batch 3b): BICEPS SPIDER CURL, CALVES (10 gastrocnemius/soleus exercises), CHEST (9 press/push-up variations) (20 exercises)
- Session 11 (Batch 4): CHEST EXERCISES (25 exercises: incline press variations, push-ups, machine/flat press, fly variations)
- Session 12 (Batch 5): CHEST (3), HAMSTRINGS (11), QUADRICEPS (11) - 25 exercises
- Session 13 (Batch 6): QUADRICEPS (25 exercises: lunges, squats, lateral movements, single-leg variations)
- Session 14 (Batch 7): QUADRICEPS (6: split squats, step-ups, lunges, leg extensions), CORE (5: superman variations, back raise), SHOULDERS (14: presses & raises) - 25 exercises
- Session 15 (Batch 8): SHOULDERS (11: lateral raises, rear delt variations, external rotations), TRICEPS (14: dips, extensions, kickbacks, close-grip bench) - 25 exercises

## Enhanced Column Structure (16 Columns)

This version includes:
- **Level**: Beginner/Intermediate/Advanced
- **Body Region**: Upper / Lower / Core
- **Movement Pattern**: Push / Pull / Static
- **Modality**: Free Weight / Cables / Fixed Resistance / Bodyweight (full text, no abbreviations)
- **Video Links**: ExRx (static pages), JEFIT (static pages), CoachWhitt (placeholder)

**Note**: The "Joint" column has been removed as redundant with the "Type" column (Compound = Multi-joint, Isolation = Single-joint)

---

## CHEST EXERCISES

| Exercise | Primary Muscles | Secondary Muscles | Type | Equipment | Level | Body Region | Movement | Modality | Steps for Beginners | Advanced Key Points | Scientific Reference | Sports Tags | ExRx Video | JEFIT Video | CoachWhitt Video |
|----------|----------------|-------------------|------|-----------|-------|-------------|----------|----------|---------------------|---------------------|---------------------|-------------|------------|-------------|------------------|
| Barbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal, clavicular) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii | Compound | Barbell, Bench | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lie flat on bench, eyes under bar 2. Grip bar slightly wider than shoulders 3. Unrack and lower to mid-chest 4. Press to lockout 5. Maintain 5-point contact | 1. Retract scapulae throughout 2. Leg drive initiates press 3. Bar path slightly diagonal 4. Maintain wrist alignment 5. Control eccentric 2-3 sec | Lehman GJ (2005). The influence of grip width and forearm pronation/supination on upper-body myoelectric activity. J Strength Cond Res 19(3):587-591 | Powerlifting, Football, Rugby, Wrestling | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/BBBenchPress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/2/barbell-bench-press | [TBD] |
| Incline Dumbbell Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular emphasis) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Serratus Anterior | Compound | Dumbbells, Incline Bench (30-45°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Set bench to 30-45 degrees 2. Start with dumbbells at shoulder height 3. Press up and slightly together 4. Lower with control to stretch 5. Avoid excessive arch | 1. Greater ROM than barbell 2. Converge at top (slight medial rotation) 3. Elbows 45° from torso 4. Maintain shoulder depression 5. Unilateral stabilization demands | Barnett C et al. (1995). Effects of variations of the bench press exercise on the EMG activity of five shoulder muscles. J Strength Cond Res 9(4):222-227 | Basketball, Volleyball, Tennis, Boxing | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralClavicular/DBInclineBenchPress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/31/dumbbell-incline-bench-press | [TBD] |
| Dips (Chest Emphasis) | Pectoralis Major (sternal), Triceps | Anterior Deltoid, Serratus Anterior | Compound | Parallel Bars or Dip Station | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Grip bars shoulder-width 2. Lean forward 20-30° 3. Lower until stretch felt 4. Press up maintaining lean 5. Avoid shoulder impingement | 1. Forward lean activates chest 2. Elbows flared 45° 3. Full ROM crucial 4. Scapular protraction at top 5. Control descent 3 sec | Gołaś A et al. (2017). Optimizing half squat postactivation potentiation with handheld loading. J Strength Cond Res 31(11):3033-3038 | Gymnastics, CrossFit, MMA, Climbing | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/BWChestDip | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/24/dip | [TBD] |
| Cable Crossover (Mid-Height) | Pectoralis Major (sternal, clavicular) | Anterior Deltoid | Isolation | Cable Machine | Advanced | Upper | Push | Cables | 1. Set cables at shoulder height 2. Step forward, slight bend 3. Bring hands together 4. Squeeze at midline 5. Return with control | 1. Constant tension advantage 2. Hands cross over midline 3. Slight internal rotation 4. Maintain posture/avoid leaning 5. Peak contraction hold 1-2 sec | Welsch EA et al. (2005). Electromyographic activity of the pectoralis major and anterior deltoid muscles during three upper-body lifts. J Strength Cond Res 19(2):449-452 | Bodybuilding, Aesthetic Sports | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/CBStandingFly | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/1057/cable-mid-chest-crossover | [TBD] |
| Push-Ups (Standard) | Pectoralis Major, Triceps | Anterior Deltoid, Serratus Anterior, Core | Compound | Bodyweight | Beginner | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Hands shoulder-width 2. Body straight line 3. Lower chest to floor 4. Press to full extension 5. Engage core throughout | 1. Scapular protraction crucial 2. Hollow body position 3. Hands under shoulders 4. Full ROM essential 5. Tempo control (2-0-2) | Freeman S et al. (2006). Quantifying muscle patterns and spine load during various forms of the push-up. Med Sci Sports Exerc 38(3):570-577 | Military, General Fitness, Calisthenics | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/BWPushup | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/47/push-up | [TBD] |
| Decline Barbell Press | Pectoralis Major (lower sternal emphasis) | Triceps, Anterior Deltoid | Compound | Barbell, Decline Bench (15-30°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Secure feet in decline bench 2. Unrack with spotter 3. Lower to lower chest 4. Press to lockout 5. Controlled tempo | 1. Emphasizes lower pec fibers 2. Shorter ROM than flat 3. Reduced shoulder stress 4. Higher loads possible 5. Avoid excessive decline | Glass SC, Armstrong T (1997). Electromyographical activity of the pectoralis muscle during incline and decline bench presses. J Strength Cond Res 11(3):163-167 | Powerlifting, Bodybuilding | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/BBDeclineBenchPress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/5/barbell-decline-bench-press | [TBD] |
| Dumbbell Flyes | Pectoralis Major | Anterior Deltoid, Biceps Brachii (long head) | Isolation | Dumbbells, Flat Bench | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Start with dumbbells pressed up 2. Slight elbow bend (fixed) 3. Arc out to sides 4. Deep stretch position 5. Return same arc path | 1. Fixed elbow angle throughout 2. Eccentric 3-4 sec critical 3. Stretch emphasis exercise 4. Lighter loads required 5. Avoid going too deep | Welsch EA et al. (2005). EMG activity of pectoralis major during upper-body lifts. J Strength Cond Res 19(2):449-452 | Bodybuilding, Rehabilitation | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/DBFly | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/41/dumbbell-fly | [TBD] |
| Incline Barbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular/upper chest) | Anterior Deltoids, Triceps | Compound | Barbell, Incline Bench (30-45°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Set bench to 30-45 degrees 2. Wide overhand grip 3. Unrack with spotter 4. Lower to upper chest 5. Press to lockout | 1. 44° incline optimal for clavicular head 2. Targets upper chest fibers 3. Grip slightly wider than flat bench 4. Maintain scapular retraction 5. Controlled eccentric essential | Barnett C et al. (1995). Effects of bench press variations on EMG. J Strength Cond Res 9(4):222-227 | Bodybuilding, Strength Sports | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralClavicular/BBInclineBenchPress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/8/barbell-incline-bench-press | [TBD] |
| Machine Chest Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal) | Triceps, Anterior Deltoids | Compound | Chest Press Machine | Beginner | Upper | Push | Fixed Resistance | 1. Adjust seat height (handles at chest) 2. Grip handles 3. Press until arms extended 4. Control return to start 5. Avoid locking elbows | 1. Fixed movement path 2. Reduced stabilizer demand 3. Safe for beginners 4. Consistent tension on chest 5. Can load heavier safely | Schoenfeld BJ et al. (2010). Mechanisms of muscle hypertrophy. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2857-2872 | Beginner Training, Bodybuilding, Rehabilitation | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/LVChestPress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/163/machine-bench-press | [TBD] |
| Alternating Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Core stabilizers (anti-rotation) | Compound | Dumbbells, Flat Bench | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lie supine on flat bench holding dumbbells at chest level 2. Position feet flat on floor, maintain neutral spine 3. Start with both dumbbells at chest, elbows at 45° 4. Press ONE dumbbell up to full elbow extension 5. Lower that dumbbell while pressing opposite dumbbell up 6. Continue alternating in controlled rhythm 7. Maintain constant core tension to resist rotation | 1. Massive anti-rotation core demand—transverse abdominis and obliques highly active 2. Unilateral loading identifies left/right chest strength imbalances 3. Time under tension doubled per side vs bilateral pressing 4. Breathing: exhale during press, inhale during lowering of each rep 5. Perfect for athletic programs requiring rotational stability | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Fimland MS et al. (2011). Muscle activity in semi-free-weight vs machine exercises. J Strength Cond Res 25(4):1034-1039 ; Schoenfeld BJ (2010). Hypertrophy mechanisms. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2857-2872 | Baseball, American Football, Rugby, Tennis, Golf, Boxing, Wrestling, MMA | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Barbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii | Compound | Barbell, Flat Bench, Power Rack | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lie supine on flat bench, eyes under barbell 2. Grip bar slightly wider than shoulder-width 3. Unrack bar to arms extended position above chest 4. Lower bar to mid-chest with elbows at 45° 5. Touch chest lightly without bouncing 6. Press bar up to full elbow extension 7. Maintain shoulder blade retraction throughout | 1. King of upper body pressing—highest load potential for pec development 2. Bar path: slight arc from mid-chest to above shoulders at lockout 3. Leg drive: push feet into floor to maintain upper back tightness 4. Balanced sternal and clavicular head activation on flat angle 5. Greater triceps activation than dumbbell press due to fixed hand position | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench inclinations and pectoralis EMG. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Schick EE et al. (2010). Smith machine vs free weight. J Strength Cond Res 24(3):779-784 | American Football, Rugby, Wrestling, Powerlifting, Strongman, MMA, Boxing | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Chest Press (Machine) | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii | Compound | Chest Press Machine | Beginner | Upper | Push | Machine | 1. Adjust seat height so handles align with mid-chest 2. Sit with back flat against pad, feet on floor 3. Grip handles with neutral or pronated grip 4. Press handles forward to full arm extension 5. Pause briefly at full extension 6. Lower with control to starting position 7. Avoid allowing weight stack to touch between reps | 1. Fixed path eliminates stabilizer recruitment—pure pec isolation 2. Safer for high-rep training to failure without spotter 3. Allows higher loads than dumbbells due to stability 4. Perfect for beginners learning pressing mechanics before free weights 5. Use for drop sets and metabolic stress training | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Fimland MS et al. (2011). Machine vs free weight muscle activity. J Strength Cond Res 25(4):1034-1039 ; Schick EE et al. (2010). Machine chest press mechanics. J Strength Cond Res 24(3):779-784 | General Fitness, Bodybuilding, Rehabilitation, Beginner Strength Training | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Decline Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head - emphasized), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid (reduced vs flat/incline), Triceps Brachii | Compound | Dumbbells, Decline Bench | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Secure feet under leg supports on decline bench 2. Lie back on decline (15-30° angle) holding dumbbells 3. Position dumbbells at chest level, elbows at 45° 4. Press dumbbells up to full extension above lower chest 5. Lower with control allowing stretch below chest level 6. Press back up, optionally bring dumbbells together at top 7. Maintain constant tension on pectorals throughout | 1. Maximal sternal (lower) pectoralis major activation due to decline angle 2. Minimal anterior deltoid involvement compared to flat/incline 3. Dumbbells allow greater ROM than barbell (descend below chest) 4. Adduction at top (bringing DBs together) increases pec contraction 5. Enhanced safety vs decline barbell—drop dumbbells if needed | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench angle effects on pectoralis EMG. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Lauver JD et al. (2016). Bench angle muscular activation. Eur J Sport Sci 16(3):309-316 | Bodybuilding, American Football, Rugby, Wrestling, Powerlifting, MMA | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Decline Smith Machine Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head - emphasized), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid (reduced), Triceps Brachii | Compound | Smith Machine, Decline Bench | Beginner | Upper | Push | Machine | 1. Position decline bench under Smith machine bar 2. Secure feet under leg supports 3. Lie back on decline, position bar above lower chest 4. Unrack bar and lower to lower chest with control 5. Press bar up to full extension 6. Rerack at any point using safety hooks 7. Maintain shoulder blade retraction throughout | 1. Fixed vertical path allows focus on lower pec contraction 2. Safer for training to failure without spotter (rerack anytime) 3. Reduced stabilizer demand vs free weight decline press 4. Great for isolating sternal pectoralis major fibers 5. Linear bar path may not suit all individual biomechanics | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench angle effects. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Schick EE et al. (2010). Smith machine vs free weight. J Strength Cond Res 24(3):779-784 | Bodybuilding, General Fitness, Powerlifting Assistance, Rehabilitation | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Stabilizer muscles (rotator cuff) | Compound | Dumbbells, Flat Bench | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Sit on flat bench holding dumbbells on thighs 2. Lie back while bringing dumbbells to chest level 3. Position feet flat on floor, maintain neutral spine 4. Press dumbbells up to full extension above chest 5. Lower with control, allowing elbows to descend below chest level 6. Press back up, optionally bring dumbbells together at top 7. Maintain shoulder blade retraction throughout | 1. Greater pectoralis activation than barbell due to increased ROM 2. Descend dumbbells below chest level for deep stretch 3. Adduction at top (bringing DBs together) maximizes pec contraction 4. Significantly higher anterior deltoid and stabilizer activation vs barbell 5. Independent arm movement identifies left/right strength imbalances | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Fimland MS et al. (2011). Free weight vs machine muscle activity. J Strength Cond Res 25(4):1034-1039 ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Pectoralis EMG analysis. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 | American Football, Rugby, Wrestling, Baseball, MMA, Bodybuilding, General Fitness | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Dumbbell Push-Up | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Core stabilizers (rectus abdominis, obliques), Serratus Anterior | Compound | Dumbbells (hexagonal preferred) | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Place two hexagonal dumbbells on floor shoulder-width apart 2. Grip dumbbell handles in push-up position 3. Body in straight plank: shoulders to ankles in line 4. Lower body by bending elbows to 90° 5. Descend slightly deeper than normal push-up (below hand level) 6. Press back up to full elbow extension 7. Maintain rigid core throughout movement | 1. Elevated hand position increases ROM vs floor push-ups 2. Neutral grip (palms facing) reduces shoulder stress vs pronated 3. Enhanced grip strength and forearm activation from holding dumbbells 4. Unstable surface increases core and stabilizer demand 5. Progress to single-arm variations for anti-rotation core work | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Kim YS et al. (2016). Push-up variations muscle activity. J Phys Ther Sci 28(5):1349-1352 ; Schoenfeld BJ (2010). Hypertrophy mechanisms. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2857-2872 | MMA, Wrestling, Gymnastics, Calisthenics, American Football, Rugby, General Fitness | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Elevated Push-Up | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head - emphasized), Pectoralis Major (sternal head) | Anterior Deltoid (increased activation), Triceps Brachii, Core stabilizers | Compound | Elevated Surface (bench, box, or platform) | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Place hands on elevated surface (bench/box) shoulder-width apart 2. Walk feet back to create straight plank position 3. Body angle: feet lower than hands (incline position) 4. Lower chest toward elevated surface by bending elbows 5. Descend until chest nearly touches surface 6. Press back up to full elbow extension 7. Maintain rigid core and neutral spine | 1. Incline angle emphasizes clavicular (upper) pectoralis major 2. Higher elevation = greater upper pec/anterior deltoid activation 3. Regression for beginners: higher surface = easier resistance 4. Progress by lowering surface height until reaching floor push-ups 5. Narrow hand position increases pectoralis and triceps activation | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Kim YS et al. (2016). Push-up angle effects. J Phys Ther Sci 28(5):1349-1352 ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench angle and pectoralis activation. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 | General Fitness, Bodyweight Training, Rehabilitation, MMA, Wrestling, Gymnastics | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Flat Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (sternal head), Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii | Compound | Barbell, Flat Bench, Power Rack | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lie supine on flat bench with eyes under barbell 2. Grip barbell slightly wider than shoulder-width 3. Unrack bar to arms extended above chest 4. Lower bar to mid-chest with controlled tempo 5. Touch chest lightly, maintain tension 6. Press bar up to full extension 7. Rerack safely with spotter or safety pins | 1. Balanced activation of both sternal and clavicular pectoralis heads 2. Retract and depress shoulder blades before unracking 3. Bar path: slight arc from mid-chest to above shoulders 4. Leg drive: push feet into floor for upper back stability 5. Elbow angle: 45° from torso (not 90° flared out) | Solari F & Burns B (2023). Pectoralis Major Anatomy. StatPearls ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench press EMG analysis. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Trebs AA et al. (2010). Chest press angle EMG. J Strength Cond Res 24(7):1925-1930 | Powerlifting, American Football, Rugby, Wrestling, Strongman, MMA, General Strength | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Pec Deck Machine | Pectoralis Major | Anterior Deltoid | Isolation | Pec Deck Machine | Beginner | Upper | Push | Fixed Resistance | 1. Adjust seat height (arms level) 2. Place forearms on pads 3. Bring arms together 4. Squeeze at midline 5. Return with control | 1. Fixed movement path 2. Reduced stabilizer demand 3. Easy to reach failure safely 4. Consistent tension 5. Ideal for burnout sets | Welsch EA et al. (2005). EMG activity during chest exercises. J Strength Cond Res 19(2):449-452 | Bodybuilding, Beginner Training, Rehabilitation | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/PectoralSternal/LVPecDeckFly | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/45/machine-fly | [TBD] |

## BACK EXERCISES

| Exercise | Primary Muscles | Secondary Muscles | Type | Equipment | Level | Body Region | Movement | Modality | Steps for Beginners | Advanced Key Points | Scientific Reference | Sports Tags | ExRx Video | JEFIT Video | CoachWhitt Video |
|----------|----------------|-------------------|------|-----------|-------|-------------|----------|----------|---------------------|---------------------|---------------------|-------------|------------|-------------|------------------|
| Conventional Deadlift | Erector Spinae, Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings | Latissimus Dorsi, Trapezius, Quadriceps, Forearms | Compound | Barbell | Advanced | Upper | Pull | Free Weight | 1. Feet hip-width, bar over mid-foot 2. Hip hinge, grip bar 3. Chest up, neutral spine 4. Drive through floor 5. Lockout hips and knees | 1. Maintain neutral spine throughout 2. Lats engaged (bend bar cue) 3. Bar path vertical 4. Hip hinge pattern crucial 5. Proper bracing essential | Swinton PA et al. (2011). A biomechanical analysis of straight and hexagonal barbell deadlifts. J Strength Cond Res 25(7):2000-2009 | Powerlifting, Strongman, General Strength | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/ErectorSpinae/BBDeadlift | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/14/barbell-deadlift | [TBD] |
| Pull-Ups (Wide Grip) | Latissimus Dorsi | Biceps Brachii, Lower Trapezius, Rhomboids, Posterior Deltoid | Compound | Pull-Up Bar | Intermediate | Upper | Pull | Bodyweight | 1. Grip bar wider than shoulders 2. Hang with arms extended 3. Pull chest to bar 4. Squeeze scapulae 5. Lower with control | 1. Initiate with scapular depression 2. Lead with chest, not chin 3. Full ROM essential 4. Avoid kipping 5. Dead hang each rep | Youdas JW et al. (2010). Surface EMG comparison during pull-up variations. J Strength Cond Res 24(7):1941-1949 | Gymnastics, CrossFit, Military, Climbing | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/LatissimusDorsi/BWPullup | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/46/pull-up | [TBD] |
| Barbell Bent-Over Row | Latissimus Dorsi, Rhomboids, Middle Trapezius | Erector Spinae, Posterior Deltoid, Biceps | Compound | Barbell | Intermediate | Upper | Pull | Free Weight | 1. Hip hinge 45° forward 2. Grip shoulder-width or wider 3. Row to lower ribcage 4. Squeeze scapulae 5. Lower with control | 1. Maintain hip hinge position 2. Horizontal pull emphasis 3. Elbow path crucial 4. Avoid excessive momentum 5. Retract and depress scapulae | Fenwick CM et al. (2009). Comparison of different rowing exercises. Med Sci Sports Exerc 41(5):1265-1270 | Rowing, Powerlifting, General Strength | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/BackGeneral/BBBentOverRow | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/3/barbell-bent-over-row | [TBD] |
| Lat Pulldown (Front) | Latissimus Dorsi | Biceps Brachii, Lower Trapezius, Rhomboids | Compound | Lat Pulldown Machine | Beginner | Upper | Pull | Fixed Resistance | 1. Secure thighs under pad 2. Grip bar wider than shoulders 3. Pull to upper chest 4. Lean back slightly (15°) 5. Control ascent | 1. Initiate with scapular depression 2. Pull to clavicles, not waist 3. Elbows down and back 4. Avoid excessive lean 5. Squeeze lats at bottom | Andersen V et al. (2014). Muscle activation during pull-up variations. Sit with feet on platform 2. Grip handle, slight knee bend 3. Row to abdomen 4. Squeeze scapulae together 5. Extend arms fully | 1. Torso upright throughout 2. Drive elbows back 3. Scapular retraction emphasis 4. Avoid excessive trunk movement 5. Full stretch at extension | Fenwick CM et al. (2009). EMG comparison of rowing exercises. Med Sci Sports Exerc 41(5):1265-1270 | Rowing, Bodybuilding, Rehabilitation | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/BackGeneral/CBSeatedRow | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/160/seated-cable-row | [TBD] |
| Single-Arm Dumbbell Row | Latissimus Dorsi, Rhomboids, Trapezius | Posterior Deltoid, Biceps, Core (anti-rotation) | Compound | Dumbbell, Bench | Intermediate | Upper | Pull | Free Weight | 1. Hand and knee on bench 2. Dumbbell in opposite hand 3. Row to hip/ribcage 4. Squeeze at top 5. Lower with control | 1. Unilateral anti-rotation demand 2. Greater ROM than barbell 3. Avoid trunk rotation 4. Pull to hip, not shoulder 5. Maintain neutral spine | Lehman GJ et al. (2004). Muscle recruitment patterns during the prone row. | Barbell Overhead Press | Anterior/Medial Deltoid | Triceps, Upper Trapezius, Serratus Anterior, Upper Pectoralis | Compound | Barbell | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Bar at clavicles, hands shoulder-width 2. Brace core, press overhead 3. Lock out fully 4. Lower to clavicles 5. Maintain vertical bar path | 1. Full overhead lockout essential 2. Avoid excessive low back arch 3. Squeeze glutes for stability 4. Bar path around face 5. Greater stabilizer demand than dumbbell | Saeterbakken AH et al. (2013). Effects of body position and loading modality on muscle activity during shoulder exercises. | Dumbbell Lateral Raise | Medial Deltoid | Anterior Deltoid, Supraspinatus, Upper Trapezius | Isolation | Dumbbells | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand with dumbbells at sides 2. Slight forward lean 3. Raise to shoulder height 4. Slight bend in elbows 5. Lower with control | 1. Neutral or internal rotation thumb position 2. Control eccentric 3 sec 3. Stop at shoulder height 4. Avoid momentum/swinging 5. Scapular stability crucial | Campos YAC et al. (2020). Different shoulder exercises and EMG activity. Reduced stability demand 4. Converge slightly at top 5. Controlled tempo (2-0-2-1) | Saeterbakken AH et al. (2013). Muscle activity during shoulder press variations. | Front Raise | Anterior Deltoid | Medial Deltoid, Clavicular Pectoralis, Serratus Anterior | Isolation | Dumbbells or Weight Plate | Beginner | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand with weights at thighs 2. Raise to eye level 3. Slight bend in elbows 4. Control descent 5. Alternate or both arms | 1. Stop at shoulder/eye level 2. Avoid momentum 3. Eccentric control crucial 4. Neutral or pronated grip 5. Often redundant with pressing | Campos YAC et al. (2020). EMG activity in shoulder exercises. J Hum Kinet 75:5-14 | Bodybuilding, Shoulder Aesthetics | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/DeltoidAnterior/DBFrontRaise | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/42/front-dumbbell-raise | [TBD] |
| Reverse Pec Deck | Posterior Deltoid | Rhomboids, Middle Trapezius, Infraspinatus | Isolation | Pec Deck Machine (Reverse) | Beginner | Upper | Pull | Fixed Resistance | 1. Face machine, chest on pad 2. Grip handles 3. Fly outward to horizontal 4. Squeeze scapulae 5. Return with control | 1. Fixed movement path 2. Excellent rear delt isolation 3. Minimal momentum possible 4. Protract to stretch 5. Retract to contract | Schoenfeld BJ et al. (2013). Muscle activation patterns. J Strength Cond Res 27(8):2240-2249 | Bodybuilding, Shoulder Balance | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/DeltoidPosterior/LVRearDeltFly | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/163/reverse-pec-deck | [TBD] |
| Pike Push-Up | Anterior/Medial Deltoid | Triceps, Serratus Anterior, Upper Trapezius | Compound | Bodyweight | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Start in downward dog position 2. Hands shoulder-width 3. Lower head to floor 4. Press back up 5. Maintain pike position | 1. Progression to handstand push-up 2. Greater shoulder emphasis than regular push-up 3. Scalable by elevation 4. Core stability demand 5. Vertical pressing pattern | Borreani S et al. (2015). Muscle activation during push-up variations. J Strength Cond Res 29(1):265-275 | Calisthenics, CrossFit, Gymnastics | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/DeltoidAnterior/BWPikePushup | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/1127/pike-push-up | [TBD] |
| Landmine Press | Anterior/Medial Deltoid | Triceps, Serratus Anterior, Core (anti-rotation) | Compound | Barbell in Landmine or Corner | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Load one end of barbell 2. Press at angle overhead 3. Slight rotation acceptable 4. Control descent 5. Alternate sides | 1. Shoulder-friendly pressing angle 2. Anti-rotation core demand 3. Unilateral instability 4. Natural arc motion 5. Excellent for shoulder health | Calatayud J et al. (2016). Muscle activity levels in shoulder exercises. Sports Biomech 15(1):42-53 | Functional Training, Rehabilitation, Athletic Performance | https://exrx.net/WeightExercises/DeltoidAnterior/BBLandminePress | https://www.jefit.com/exercises/1188/landmine-press | [TBD] |
| Face Pulls with External Rotation | Posterior Deltoid, Infraspinatus, Teres Minor | Rhomboids, Middle Trapezius, Supraspinatus | Isolation | Cable with Rope Attachment | Beginner | Upper | Pull | Cables | 1. Cable at face height 2. Pull rope to face 3. Externally rotate at end 4. Hands beside ears 5. Control return | 1. Critical for shoulder health 2. External rotation component key 3. High volume tolerated 4. Scapular retraction emphasis 5. Pull apart rope at finish | Schoenfeld BJ et al. (2013). Training protocols and muscle activation. Keep front knee aligned over ankle, avoid knee passing toes 5. Push through front heel to return to starting position 6. Repeat on opposite leg, alternating sides | 1. Load progression: Start bodyweight → light dumbbells (5-10 lbs) → moderate (15-25 lbs) → heavy (30+ lbs) 2. Torso position: Maintain upright posture—forward lean increases glute/hamstring, vertical torso emphasizes quads 3. Knee tracking: Front knee tracks in line with 2nd-3rd toe throughout movement 4. Tempo control: 2-second descent, 1-second pause at bottom, 1-second ascent maximizes quad activation 5. | Dumbbell Reverse Lunge | Quadriceps (Rectus Femoris, Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis), Gluteus Maximus (stationary limb emphasis) | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris - lead limb emphasis), Gluteus Medius, Adductors, Erector Spinae | Compound | Dumbbells (pair) | Beginner | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand upright holding dumbbells at sides, feet hip-width apart 2. Step backward with right leg approximately 2-3 feet 3. Lower body by bending both knees until front thigh is parallel to floor 4. Keep front knee aligned over ankle, back knee hovers above floor 5. Push through front heel to return to starting position 6. Repeat on opposite leg, alternating sides | 1. Biomechanical advantage: Reverse lunge reduces anterior knee shear force vs forward lunge—safer for knee health 2. Stationary limb focus: Front (stationary) leg drives movement—push floor away to emphasize quad activation 3. Back leg role: Moving leg primarily for balance—keep weight distribution 70% front/30% back 4. Torso angle: Slight forward lean (10-15°) increases glute activation in stationary limb 5. Tempo variation: Pause 2 seconds at bottom position to eliminate momentum and maximize quad time-under-tension | Boullosa D et al. (2024). The Reverse Lunge: A Descriptive Electromyographic Study. Applied Sciences 14(24):11480. DOI: 10.3390/app142411480; Muyor JM et al. (2020). EMG during lunge variations. PLoS One 15(4):e0230841; Stastny P et al. (2015). Dumbbell-carrying position effects. J Hum Kinet 49:59-67. DOI: 10.1515/hukin-2015-0107 | Basketball, Volleyball, Tennis, Soccer, American Football, Rugby, Track & Field, Hockey, Lacrosse, Baseball, General Fitness, Rehabilitation | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Dumbbell Side Lunge (Lateral Lunge) | Gluteus Medius (emphasized), Adductors (Adductor Longus, Adductor Magnus, Gracilis), Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis) | Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings, Gastrocnemius/Soleus | Compound | Dumbbell (single) or Dumbbells (pair) | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand upright holding dumbbell at chest level (goblet style) or dumbbells at sides 2. Take wide step laterally (to the side) with right leg 3. Shift weight to right leg, bend right knee while keeping left leg straight 4. Lower hips back and down until right thigh is parallel to floor 5. Push through right heel to return to starting position 6. Repeat on opposite side, alternating | 1. Lead leg mechanics: Hip hinge pattern—sit back into hip, not forward over knee 2. Straight leg tension: Passive leg adductor elongates eccentrically, then shortens concentrically to bring legs together 3. Knee tracking: Lead knee tracks over 2nd toe—avoid knee valgus (collapsing inward) 4. Foot position: Lead foot points forward or slightly outward (5-10°)—never inward 5. Load progression: Goblet hold (single DB at chest) → dual DBs at sides → dual DBs at shoulders for progression | Muyor JM et al. (2020). EMG activity during lateral movements. PLoS One 15(4):e0230841; Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal muscle activation during exercises. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Ekstrom RA et al. (2007). Surface EMG analysis. | Dumbbell Split Squat (Bulgarian Split Squat) | Quadriceps (Rectus Femoris, Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis), Gluteus Maximus | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Gluteus Medius, Erector Spinae, Core stabilizers | Compound | Dumbbells (pair), elevated surface (bench 15-24 inches) | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand 2-3 feet in front of elevated bench, holding dumbbells at sides 2. Place top of right foot on bench behind you (laces down) 3. Lower body by bending front knee until thigh is parallel to floor 4. Keep front knee aligned over ankle, torso upright 5. Push through front heel to return to starting position 6. Complete all reps on one leg, then switch | 1. Trunk angle manipulation: Forward lean (15-20°) increases glute/hamstring; upright torso emphasizes quads 2. Front foot position: 2-3 feet from bench—closer = more quad, farther = more glute/hamstring 3. Rear foot placement: Laces down on bench (not ball of foot) reduces rear leg contribution 4. Load distribution: 90% weight on front leg—back leg is for balance only 5. Deficit variation: Elevate front foot 2-4 inches to increase ROM and quad stretch | Slater LV et al. (2025). Targeted muscle activation in Bulgarian split squat variations. BMC Sports Sci Med Rehabil 17:28. DOI: 10.1186/s13102-025-01306-z; Yavuz HU et al. (2014). Muscle activation in Bulgarian squat. Int J Sports Med 36(10):771-777. DOI: 10.1055/s-0034-1382016; Saeterbakken AH et al. (2019). Muscle activity of Bulgarian squat. | Dumbbell Squat (Goblet Squat) | Quadriceps (Vastus Medialis, Vastus Lateralis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Hamstrings, Adductors, Erector Spinae, Core stabilizers | Compound | Dumbbell (single, goblet position) or Dumbbells (pair, at sides) | Beginner | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Hold single dumbbell vertically at chest level, hands cupping top end (goblet position) 2. Stand with feet shoulder-width apart, toes pointed slightly outward (5-15°) 3. Initiate movement by pushing hips back and bending knees 4. Lower body until thighs are parallel to floor or below (full depth if mobility allows) 5. Keep chest up, elbows inside knees at bottom 6. Push through heels to return to standing position | 1. Goblet position advantage: DB at chest encourages upright torso—reduces forward lean common in back squats 2. Elbow-knee contact: At bottom, use elbows to gently push knees outward to activate glute medius 3. Depth progression: Quarter squat → parallel → ass-to-grass as mobility improves 4. Breathing: Inhale at top + brace core, hold breath during descent, exhale past sticking point on ascent 5. Load ceiling: Goblet squat limited by upper body—transition to barbell front squat when exceeding 60-70 lbs | Makaruk B et al. (2021). Goblet squat vs landmine squat. J Strength Cond Res 35(8):2141-2148. DOI: 10.1519/JSC.0000000000003109; Yavuz HU et al. (2015). Kinematic and EMG during front and back squat. J Sports Sci 33(16):1674-1684; Muyor JM et al. (2020). | Dumbbell Step-Up | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis), Gluteus Maximus | Gluteus Medius, Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Adductors, Gastrocnemius/Soleus | Compound | Dumbbells (pair), elevated platform or box (12-24 inches) | Beginner | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand facing elevated box/platform (12-20 inches), holding dumbbells at sides 2. Place right foot entirely on box surface 3. Push through right heel to lift body onto box 4. Bring left foot up to meet right foot on box 5. Step down with left foot first, then right foot 6. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch to left | 1. Box height selection: Knee angle at 90° when foot is on box = optimal quad activation 2. Drive mechanics: Push through heel of elevated foot—avoid pushing off trailing foot 3. Torso position: Slight forward lean (10-15°) increases glute engagement 4. Eccentric control: 2-3 second descent when stepping down—eccentric quad overload 5. Advanced variation: Lateral step-up increases glute medius activation vs standard forward step-up | Muyor JM et al. (2020). EMG during step-up exercises. PLoS One 15(4):e0230841. DOI: 10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Ayotte NW et al. (2007). EMG analysis of gluteus medius and maximus. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 37(5):264-272; Andersen V et al. (2018). EMG during loaded step-ups. | Dumbbell Walking Lunge | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Gluteus Medius, Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Adductors, Erector Spinae, Core stabilizers | Compound | Dumbbells (pair) | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand upright holding dumbbells at sides, feet hip-width apart 2. Step forward with right leg approximately 2-3 feet 3. Lower body by bending both knees to 90 degrees 4. Push through right heel and bring left leg forward into next lunge 5. Continue alternating legs while moving forward 6. Maintain upright torso throughout movement | 1. Step length: 2.5-3 feet stride length = optimal quad/glute balance—shorter emphasizes quads, longer emphasizes glutes 2. Continuous motion: Unlike stationary lunges, maintain forward momentum—builds dynamic stability 3. Knee tracking: Front knee tracks over 2nd-3rd toe throughout movement—never collapse inward 4. Core engagement: Anti-rotation demand high—brace core to prevent torso twisting 5. Breathing pattern: Inhale during step/descent, exhale during drive/next step | Stastny P et al. (2015). | Forward Lunge | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Gluteus Medius, Adductors, Erector Spinae | Compound | Bodyweight | Beginner | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Stand upright with feet hip-width apart, hands on hips 2. Step forward with right leg approximately 2-3 feet 3. Lower body by bending both knees until front thigh is parallel to floor 4. Keep front knee aligned over ankle, back knee hovers above floor 5. Push through front heel to return to starting position 6. Repeat on opposite leg, alternating sides | 1. Progression pathway: Bodyweight → holding light dumbbells → barbell front rack → barbell back rack 2. Knee position: Front knee stays behind toes—if knee passes toes, increase step length 3. Torso angle: Upright (vertical) = quad emphasis; forward lean (15-20°) = glute/hamstring emphasis 4. Tempo manipulation: Pause 2 seconds at bottom to eliminate momentum and maximize quad activation 5. Balance challenge: Narrow step width (single track) increases balance demand vs wide step (dual track) | Muyor JM et al. (2020). EMG during forward lunge. PLoS One 15(4):e0230841. | Lateral Barbell Squat | Gluteus Medius (emphasized), Adductors (Adductor Longus, Adductor Magnus), Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis) | Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings, Erector Spinae | Compound | Barbell | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Position barbell across upper back (high bar position), feet shoulder-width apart 2. Take wide lateral step to right (2-3 feet), keeping left leg straight 3. Shift weight to right leg, bend right knee and hip 4. Lower hips back and down until right thigh is parallel to floor 5. Push through right heel to return to center starting position 6. Repeat on opposite side, alternating | 1. Stance width: 2.5-3 feet lateral step = optimal adductor stretch and glute medius activation 2. Straight leg tension: Non-working leg remains straight with adductor actively lengthening 3. Hip hinge pattern: Sit back into hip—avoid forward knee drift over toes 4. Bar position: High bar (upper traps) = more upright; low bar (rear delts) = more forward lean 5. Foot angle: Working leg foot points forward or slightly outward (5-10°)—never inward | Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal muscle activation. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Muyor JM et al. Position barbell across upper back, stand to left side of elevated box 2. Place right foot entirely on box surface (lateral step) 3. Push through right heel to lift body onto box 4. Bring left foot up to meet right foot on box 5. Step down laterally with left foot first, then right foot 6. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch sides | 1. Lateral emphasis: Approach box from side (not front) to maximize frontal plane movement and glute medius activation 2. Drive mechanics: Push through heel of elevated foot—minimize push from trailing foot 3. Hip stability: Focus on preventing hip drop on non-elevated side—activates glute medius maximally 4. Box height: 15-20 inches = optimal for most athletes; higher box = greater glute activation 5. Progression: Bodyweight → dumbbells → barbell → barbell with chains/bands for accommodating resistance | Muyor JM et al. (2020). EMG during step-up exercises. PLoS One 15(4):e0230841. | Lateral Squat | Gluteus Medius, Adductors (Adductor Longus, Adductor Magnus, Gracilis), Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis) | Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings, Gastrocnemius/Soleus | Compound | Bodyweight | Beginner | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Shift weight to right leg, bend right knee and push hips back 4. Lower hips until right thigh is parallel to floor, left leg remains straight 5. Push through right heel to return to center standing position 6. Repeat on opposite side, alternating | 1. Bodyweight foundation: Master this pattern before adding load (goblet DB → barbell) 2. | Leg Press | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris) | Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Adductors, Gastrocnemius/Soleus | Compound | Leg Press Machine (45° angled or horizontal) | Beginner | Lower | Push | Machine | 1. Sit in leg press machine, back flat against padded backrest 2. Place feet on platform shoulder-width apart, mid-foot position 3. Release safety handles, lower platform by bending knees to 90 degrees 4. Push through heels to extend legs, stopping just before lockout 5. Control descent back to 90-degree knee angle 6. Re-engage safety handles when set is complete | 1. Foot placement variation: High on platform = glute/hamstring emphasis; low = quad emphasis; wide = adductor involvement 2. Depth control: 90° knee angle = standard; deeper (100-110°) = greater quad activation but higher knee stress 3. Lower back safety: Maintain lumbar curve against pad—avoid "butt wink" (posterior pelvic tilt) at deep angles 4. Tempo manipulation: 3-second eccentric, 1-second pause, explosive concentric maximizes hypertrophy 5. Single-leg variation: Unilateral leg press identifies left/right imbalances and increases stability demand | Lorenzetti S et al. (2020). Evaluation of lower limb muscles' EMG during leg press. Release safety handles, bend knees to lower platform to 90-degree knee angle 4. Push through heels to extend legs, stopping just before lockout 5. Control descent back to 90-degree starting position 6. Re-engage safety handles after completing set | 1. Position shoulders under padded supports of hack squat machine, back against pad 2. Place feet on platform shoulder-width apart, mid-foot position 3. Release safety handles, bend knees to lower until thighs parallel to platform 4. Keep back flat against pad throughout movement 5. Push through heels to extend legs, stopping just before lockout 6. | Reverse Lunge | Quadriceps (Rectus Femoris, Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis), Gluteus Maximus (stationary limb emphasis) | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris - lead limb emphasis), Gluteus Medius, Adductors, Erector Spinae | Compound | Bodyweight | Beginner | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. | Single-Arm Barbell Side Squat | Gluteus Medius (emphasized), Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis), Adductors (Adductor Magnus, Adductor Longus) | Gluteus Maximus, Core stabilizers (anti-rotation: Obliques, Transverse Abdominis), Erector Spinae, Hamstrings | Compound | Barbell (landmine setup or one end on floor) | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lower until right thigh parallel to floor, maintain barbell at shoulder 6. Push through right heel to return to center starting position 7. Complete all reps on right side, then switch | 1. Core bracing: Engage core before each rep—resist rotation toward loaded side 3. Hip stability: Prevent hip drop on non-working side—activates glute medius maximally 4. Barbell path: Keep barbell close to body—farther away increases core demand exponentially 5. | Single-Leg Barbell Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius (emphasized) | Hamstrings, Core stabilizers (anti-rotation, anti-lateral flexion), Erector Spinae, Adductors | Compound | Barbell | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Position barbell across upper back (high bar position) 2. Stand on right leg, left leg extended forward or held behind (counterbalance) 3. Initiate descent by pushing hips back and bending right knee 4. Lower as deep as mobility allows while maintaining balance 5. Keep right knee tracking over toes, chest up 6. Push through right heel to return to standing 7. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch | 1. Prerequisite strength: Master bodyweight pistol squats before adding barbell 2. Balance aids: Use TRX/suspension trainer for assistance while building strength 3. Non-working leg position: Forward (pistol style) = harder balance; behind (airborne lunge) = easier balance 4. Depth targets: Quarter squat → parallel → full depth as mobility/strength improves 5. Glute medius cue: Focus on preventing hip drop on non-stance side—critical for knee health | Biscarini A et al. | Single-Leg Box Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius (emphasized) | Hamstrings, Core stabilizers, Erector Spinae, Adductors | Compound | Plyometric box or bench (12-20 inches) | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Stand on right leg in front of box (12-20 inches high), left leg hovering 2. Extend arms forward for counterbalance 3. Lower body by bending right knee and pushing hips back 4. Lightly touch box with glutes (don't sit fully) 5. Immediately drive through right heel to return to standing 6. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch | 1. Box height: 16-20 inches = beginner; 12-15 inches = intermediate; <12 inches = advanced 2. Touch-and-go: Lightly tap box—don't relax or sit down (maintains muscle tension) 3. Non-working leg: Hold forward (pistol position) or behind (airborne lunge position) 4. Knee tracking: Working knee tracks over 2nd-3rd toe—prevent knee valgus (inward collapse) 5. Progression: Bodyweight → vest → goblet DB → barbell → lower box height | Biscarini A et al. (2020). EMG during single-leg squat. Gait Posture 77:133-141; Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal activation. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Swinton PA et al. (2012). Biomechanical comparison of squat variations. J Strength Cond Res 26(12):3374-3380 | Basketball, Volleyball, Track & Field, Soccer, American Football, Rugby, Tennis, Skiing, Snowboarding, Gymnastics, General Athletic Performance | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Single-Leg Dumbbell Box Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius | Hamstrings, Core stabilizers, Erector Spinae, Adductors | Compound | Dumbbell (single or pair), plyometric box or bench (12-20 inches) | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand on right leg in front of box (12-20 inches), holding dumbbell(s) at sides 2. Left leg extended forward for counterbalance 3. Lower body by bending right knee and pushing hips back 4. Lightly touch box with glutes without fully sitting 5. Drive through right heel to return to standing 6. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch | 1. Load options: Single DB (goblet hold at chest) = easier balance; dual DBs (at sides) = harder balance 2. Box height progression: High box (20 inches) → medium (16 inches) → low (12 inches) → no box (pistol squat) 3. Tempo control: 3-second descent, light touch, explosive ascent maximizes quad eccentric + concentric 4. Hip stability: Prevent hip drop on non-stance side—activates glute medius maximally 5. Regression: If balance limiting factor, use suspension trainer for hand support while building strength | Biscarini A et al. (2020). EMG of single-leg squat. Gait Posture 77:133-141; Boudreau SN et al. (2009). EMG comparison: 2-leg vs single-leg squat. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2734-2743; Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal muscle activation. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Saeterbakken AH et al. (2020). Kinematic and EMG of unilateral squats. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(13):4610 | Basketball, Volleyball, Soccer, American Football, Rugby, Tennis, Track & Field, Skiing, Snowboarding, Skateboarding, Gymnastics, General Athletic Performance | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Single-Leg Dumbbell Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Core stabilizers, Erector Spinae, Adductors, Ankle stabilizers (Tibialis Anterior, Gastrocnemius) | Compound | Dumbbell (single or pair) | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Stand on right leg, hold dumbbell at chest (goblet position) or dumbbells at sides 2. Extend left leg forward for counterbalance (pistol position) 3. Lower body by bending right knee and pushing hips back 4. Descend as deep as mobility allows while maintaining balance 5. Keep right knee tracking over toes, chest up 6. Push through right heel to return to standing 7. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch | 1. Prerequisite: Master bodyweight pistol squats before adding dumbbells 2. Load progression: Goblet hold (single DB at chest) = easier; dual DBs at sides = harder balance demand 3. Depth targets: Quarter depth → parallel → full depth (hamstring to calf contact) 4. Ankle mobility: Requires significant ankle dorsiflexion—elevate heel on plate if limited mobility 5. Assistance options: Hold TRX/suspension trainer with one hand while learning movement pattern | Biscarini A et al. (2020). EMG analysis of single-leg squat. Gait Posture 77:133-141; Boudreau SN et al. (2009). Lower extremity EMG comparison. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2734-2743; Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal muscle activation. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Saeterbakken AH et al. (2020). Kinematic and EMG of unilateral squats. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(13):4610 | Advanced Athletic Training, Skiing, Snowboarding, Skateboarding, Surfing, Gymnastics, Martial Arts, Track & Field, Basketball, Soccer, American Football, Rugby, CrossFit | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Single-Leg Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius (emphasized) | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris), Core stabilizers (anti-rotation, anti-lateral flexion), Erector Spinae, Adductors, Ankle stabilizers (Tibialis Anterior, Gastrocnemius/Soleus) | Compound | Bodyweight | Advanced | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Stand on right leg, extend left leg forward (parallel to floor if possible) 2. Extend arms forward for counterbalance 3. Lower body by bending right knee and pushing hips back 4. Descend as deep as mobility allows while keeping left leg extended 5. Keep right knee tracking over toes, chest up 6. Push through right heel to return to standing 7. Complete all reps on right leg, then switch | 1. Prerequisite strength: Must perform 15-20 bodyweight squats with excellent form before attempting pistols 2. Progression pathway: Box pistols (high box) → box pistols (low box) → full pistol → weighted pistol 3. Counterbalance: Extended leg and arms create counterweight—pull non-working leg toward chest at bottom 4. Ankle mobility: Requires extreme ankle dorsiflexion—elevate heel on small plate if limited 5. Common faults: Knee valgus (collapse inward), heel lift, forward torso lean—address each systematically | Biscarini A et al. (2020). EMG analysis of single-leg squat. Gait Posture 77:133-141; Boudreau SN et al. (2009). EMG comparison: 2-leg vs single-leg squat. J Strength Cond Res 24(10):2734-2743; Distefano LJ et al. (2009). Gluteal muscle activation during exercises. J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 39(12):532-540; Saeterbakken AH et al. (2020). Kinematic and EMG comparison of unilateral squats. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(13):4610 | Gymnastics, Martial Arts, Calisthenics, CrossFit, Advanced Athletic Training, Skiing, Snowboarding, Skateboarding, Surfing, Track & Field, Basketball, Soccer | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Smith Machine Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Medialis, Vastus Lateralis, Rectus Femoris) | Gluteus Maximus, Hamstrings, Adductors, Erector Spinae (reduced) | Compound | Smith Machine | Beginner | Lower | Push | Machine | 1. Position Smith machine bar at upper back level, load appropriate weight 2. Step under bar, position across upper back (high bar) or rear delts (low bar) 3. Unrack by rotating bar hooks upward 4. Stand with feet shoulder-width apart, toes slightly outward 5. Lower body by bending knees and hips until thighs parallel to floor 6. Push through heels to return to starting position 7. Rotate bar hooks to rerack | 1. Fixed path limitation: Smith machine reduces quad activation by 43-49% vs free weights—prioritize free squats when possible 2. Safety advantage: Can rerack at any point—useful for training to failure without spotter 3. Foot position: Can place feet forward of bar (impossible with free weight)—shifts emphasis to glutes/hamstrings 4. Biomechanical constraint: Fixed vertical path may not suit individual biomechanics—some lifters experience knee/hip discomfort 5. Use cases: Rehabilitation, beginner learning squat pattern, high-rep burnout sets, when training alone without spotter | Schick EE et al. (2010). Comparison of free weight squat to Smith machine squat using EMG. J Strength Cond Res 24(3):779-784. DOI: 10.1519/JSC.0b013e3181cc2237; Schwanbeck S et al. (2009). EMG comparison of Smith machine and free weight squat. J Strength Cond Res 23(9):2588-2591 | General Fitness, Bodybuilding, Beginner Strength Training, Rehabilitation, Powerlifting, American Football, Rugby, Track & Field | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |

| Calf Press | Gastrocnemius (medial head), Gastrocnemius (lateral head) | Soleus, Quadriceps (isometric stabilization) | Isolation | Leg Press Machine | Beginner | Lower | Push | Machine | 1. Flex ankles to let footplate move toward you for stretch 6. Press through balls of feet to extend ankles maximally 7. Hold peak contraction 1-2 seconds, return to stretch | 1. Locked knees—no flexion or quadriceps engagement 2. Machine allows 3-4x bodyweight for advanced hypertrophy 3. Use stretch reflex: pause briefly at bottom then explode 4. Single-leg variation: use 50-60% bilateral weight per leg 5. High frequency: 12-20 sets/week across 3-4 sessions optimal | Nunes JP et al. (2020). Seated vs standing calf raises. J Strength Cond Res 34(12) ; Bordoni B & Varacallo M (2023). Gastrocnemius Muscle Anatomy. StatPearls ; Schoenfeld BJ et al. (2015). Volume-equated resistance training. | Calf Raise - Seated | Gastrocnemius (medial head - reduced), Gastrocnemius (lateral head - reduced) | Soleus (NOTE: Actually PRIMARY due to knee flexion placing gastrocnemius in active insufficiency) | Isolation | Bench, Dumbbells or Weight Plates, Raised Platform | Beginner | Lower | Push | Free Weight | 1. Sit on bench with feet flat on floor 2. Place platform under balls of feet 3. Position weights on thighs just above knees 4. Drop heels to floor level for starting stretch 5. Push through balls of feet to lift heels maximally 6. Squeeze calves at peak for 1-2 seconds 7. Lower heels with control, maintain 90° knee angle | 1. 90° knee flexion isolates soleus via gastrocnemius active insufficiency 2. Soleus is Type I slow-twitch: use 15-30 reps, longer TUT vs heavy loads 3. Full ROM: 2-3 inch stretch below, 3+ inches above platform 4. Pause reps: hold 3-5 seconds at top for metabolic stress 5. Program 1:1 ratio standing to seated for balanced triceps surae development | Nunes JP et al. (2020). Seated vs standing calf raises. J Strength Cond Res 34(12) ; Avery DM & Tan JM (2023). Soleus Muscle Anatomy. StatPearls ; Remaud A et al. (2009). Knee-flexion angle effects. | Calf Raise - Standing | Gastrocnemius (medial head), Gastrocnemius (lateral head) | Soleus, Core stabilizers (erector spinae, rectus abdominis, obliques) | Isolation | Raised Platform or Step (bodyweight or add dumbbells) | Beginner | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Stand on raised platform with balls of feet, heels hanging off 2. Feet hip-width apart, toes forward or slightly outward 3. Hold wall or support for balance if needed 4. Lower heels below platform level for deep stretch 5. Push through balls of feet to rise onto tiptoes maximally 6. Squeeze calves at top for 1-2 seconds 7. Lower with control to stretched position | 1. Perfect beginner exercise: master form before adding external load 2. Progress to single-leg variation for 2x bodyweight resistance per leg 3. | Single-Leg Calf Press | Gastrocnemius (medial head), Gastrocnemius (lateral head) | Soleus, Quadriceps (isometric stabilization) | Isolation | Leg Press Machine | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Machine | 1. Sit in leg press machine with moderate weight (50-60% bilateral load) 2. Place ball of ONE foot on bottom edge of footplate, heel off 3. Other leg rests on floor or bent up off footplate 4. Extend working leg fully, maintain straight knee 5. Flex ankle to let footplate move toward you for stretch 6. Press through ball of foot to extend ankle maximally 7. Hold peak contraction, return to stretch, repeat, then switch legs | 1. Identifies and corrects bilateral strength imbalances 2. Addresses weaker leg first to prevent favoring stronger side 3. Double volume: perform same reps per leg as bilateral sets 4. Enhanced mind-muscle connection with unilateral focus 5. Lower heel below platform for deep stretch 5. Push through ball of foot to rise onto tiptoe 6. Squeeze calf hard at top for 1-2 seconds 7. Lower with control, complete set, then switch legs | 1. Doubles bodyweight resistance vs bilateral bodyweight calf raise 2. Enhanced balance requirement increases core and stabilizer activation 3. ## Session 11 (Batch 4): CHEST EXERCISES - INCLINE PRESS, PUSH-UPS, MACHINE/FLAT PRESS, FLY VARIATIONS (25 exercises)

| Exercise | Primary Muscles | Secondary Muscles | Type | Equipment | Level | Body Region | Movement | Modality | Steps for Beginners | Advanced Key Points | Scientific Reference | Sports Tags | ExRx Video | JEFIT Video | CoachWhitt Video |
|----------|----------------|-------------------|------|-----------|-------|-------------|----------|----------|---------------------|---------------------|---------------------|-------------|------------|-------------|------------------|
| Incline Alternating Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, External Oblique, Internal Oblique | Compound | Dumbbells, Incline Bench (30°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Set bench to 30° incline, sit with dumbbells on thighs 2. Lie back and position dumbbells at shoulder level, palms forward 3. Press one dumbbell upward to full extension, keep other at shoulder 4. Lower extended dumbbell with control (2-3 sec) 5. Press opposite dumbbell upward while first remains stationary 6. Continue alternating arms, maintain core tension to prevent rotation 7. After completing reps, bring dumbbells to thighs and sit up safely | 1. Alternating pattern increases external oblique activation 30-40% vs bilateral pressing 2. 30° incline produces maximal upper pec activation with minimal deltoid dominance 3. Use 1-sec concentric, 1-sec peak hold, 2-3 sec eccentric tempo 4. Maintain bilateral scapular retraction even during unilateral press 5. Core integration increases rectus abdominis/oblique activation 25-35% | Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench inclinations and pectoralis EMG. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Anderson KG & Behm DG (2005). Unilateral exercise research. Sports Med 35(1):43-53 ; Lauver JD et al. (2015). Bench angle muscular activation. Eur J Sport Sci 16(3):309-316 | Rugby, American Football, Wrestling, Boxing, MMA, Swimming, Tennis, Golf, Rock Climbing, CrossFit | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Incline Barbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Serratus Anterior | Compound | Barbell, Incline Bench (30°), Power Rack | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Set bench to 30° inside power rack with safety bars at chest level 2. Lie on bench, eyes under barbell, feet flat, natural lumbar arch 3. Grip barbell slightly wider than shoulder-width (1.5× biacromial distance) 4. Unrack barbell with arms extended, position bar over upper chest/clavicle 5. Lower barbell with control (2-3 sec) to upper chest below clavicle 6. Press barbell upward in slight backward arc to full extension 7. Complete all reps and re-rack barbell | 1. 30° incline produces 122.5% MVIC for upper pec with minimal deltoid compensation 2. Press in slight backward arc toward head to align with upper pec fiber orientation 3. 1.5-2.0× biacromial grip width maximizes pec activation 4. Maintain scapular retraction/depression throughout for stable pressing platform 5. Utilize leg drive through ground to increase pressing power | Lauver JD et al. (2015). Bench angle activation. Eur J Sport Sci 16(3):309-316 ; Rodríguez-Ridao D et al. (2020). Bench inclinations EMG. Int J Environ Res Public Health 17(19):7339 ; Lehman GJ (2005). Grip width effects. | Incline Hammer Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii (increased vs pronated), Serratus Anterior | Compound | Dumbbells, Incline Bench (30°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Set bench to 30° incline 2. Grip dumbbells with neutral/hammer grip (palms facing each other) 3. Start with dumbbells at shoulder level 4. Press upward keeping neutral grip throughout, dumbbells travel parallel 5. Lower with control to shoulder level 6. Maintain shoulder blade retraction throughout 7. Avoid wrist rotation during movement | 1. Neutral grip reduces shoulder internal rotation stress by 15-20% vs pronated grip 2. Triceps activation increases 20-25% with hammer grip vs standard pronated 3. Wrist and forearm in mechanically neutral position reduces joint stress 4. Ideal for athletes with shoulder impingement history or rotator cuff concerns 5. Upper pec activation similar to pronated grip but with improved shoulder health profile | Lehman GJ (2005). | Incline Single-Arm Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, External Oblique, Internal Oblique, Rectus Abdominis | Compound | Dumbbell, Incline Bench (30°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Lower with control (2-3 sec) to shoulder level 6. Complete all reps on right side, then switch to left 7. Maintain neutral spine throughout, avoid torso twist | 1. Non-working arm can be extended for balance or placed on hip for increased challenge 5. Superior for athletes requiring single-arm pressing power (boxing, tennis, baseball) | Anderson KG & Behm DG (2005). Unilateral resistance training. Sports Med 35(1):43-53 ; Behm DG et al. (2010). | Incline Smith Machine Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii | Compound | Smith Machine, Incline Bench (30°) | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Fixed Resistance | 1. | Incline Twisting Dumbbell Bench Press | Pectoralis Major (clavicular head, medial fibers emphasized) | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Serratus Anterior | Compound | Dumbbells, Incline Bench (30°) | Advanced | Upper | Push | Free Weight | 1. Press dumbbells upward while rotating forearms from neutral to pronated (palms forward) 4. At top position, dumbbells should be pronated and nearly touching 5. Lower with control while rotating back to neutral grip 6. Complete rotation should occur over full ROM (not at top only) 7. Maintain scapular retraction throughout | 1. | Kneeling Push-Up | Pectoralis Major | Anterior Deltoid, Triceps Brachii, Serratus Anterior, Rectus Abdominis | Compound | Bodyweight, Mat | Beginner | Upper | Push | Bodyweight | 1. Kneel on mat, hands shoulder-width apart 2. Walk hands forward until body forms straight line from head to knees 3. Engage core to maintain neutral spine 4. Lower chest toward floor by bending elbows to 90° 5. Press back up to full arm extension 6. Keep head neutral, eyes on floor 7. Complete all reps with controlled tempo | 1. Reduces load to ~45-55% of bodyweight vs standard push-up (65-75%) 2. Ideal regression for beginners unable to perform full push-ups with proper form 3. Maintain straight line from head to knees (avoid hip sag or pike) 4. Progress to standard push-up when 15-20 quality reps achieved 5. ## Session 12 (Batch 5): CHEST (3), HAMSTRINGS (11), QUADRICEPS (11) - Cable Crossovers, Pec Deck, Hip Lifts, Deadlift, Leg Curls, Squats, Lunges (25 exercises)

| Exercise | Primary Muscles | Secondary Muscles | Type | Equipment | Level | Body Region | Movement | Modality | Steps for Beginners | Advanced Key Points | Scientific Reference | Sports Tags | ExRx Video | JEFIT Video | CoachWhitt Video |
|----------|----------------|-------------------|------|-----------|-------|-------------|----------|----------|---------------------|---------------------|---------------------|-------------|------------|-------------|------------------|
| Low Cable Crossover | Pectoralis Major - Sternal Head, Pectoralis Major - Clavicular Head | Anterior Deltoid, Pectoralis Minor, Serratus Anterior | Isolation | Cable Machine | Intermediate | Upper | Push | Cables | Stand centered between cables with handles at shoulder height or above. Step forward with tension. Arms out to sides, elbows slightly bent (10-15°), palms forward. Athletic stance, one foot forward. Engage core, chest lifted. Pull handles down and across body in smooth arc to lower chest/upper abdomen. Squeeze chest at peak. Return slowly with control. | Maintain constant cable tension throughout ROM; Visualize pec fibers contracting sternum to humerus; Vary pulley height (high-low emphasizes lower pec, low-high upper pec); Avoid forward lean or momentum; Rotate palms inward at bottom, hold 1-2 seconds | Boeckh-Behrens & Buskies (2000) Fitness-Krafttraining; Schoenfeld et al. Stand feet hip-width, core braced. Take large step directly to side with one leg (2-3 feet). Keep stepping leg's foot pointing forward (not turned out). Bend knee of stepping leg while keeping opposite leg straight. Lower hips down and back as if sitting into chair on that side. Descend until working thigh approximately parallel to ground. Drive through heel of bent leg to return to start. | Side lunges create significant frontal plane loading - critical for injury prevention in multi-directional sports; Adductors and gluteus medius work extensively to control lateral motion; Keep non-working leg fully extended with foot flat on ground throughout movement; Avoid excessive forward torso lean - maintain upright posture to maximize quadriceps and glute engagement; This exercise addresses often-neglected frontal plane of motion | Myer et al. (2008) J Strength Cond Res 22(3):987-1014 DOI:10.1519/JSC.0b013e31816a86cd; McBride et al. (2010) J Sports Sci 28(11):1215-1221 DOI:10.1080/02640414.2010.499440; Muyor et al. (2020) PLOS ONE 15(4):e0230841 DOI:10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Krause Neto et al. (2020) J Sports Sci Med 19(1):91-108 PMCID:PMC7039033 | Soccer, Basketball, Tennis, American Football, Rugby, Hockey, Volleyball, Lacrosse, Skiing, Snowboarding, CrossFit, MMA | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Barbell Split Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Gluteus Medius, Adductor Magnus, Erector Spinae, Core Stabilizers | Compound | Barbell, Squat Rack | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | Position barbell across upper back in high bar position. Take large step forward with one leg (staggered stance) approximately 2-3 feet. Position rear foot on ball of foot with heel elevated. Keep both feet pointing forward, maintain hip-width spacing between feet. Lower body straight down by bending both knees. Descend until front thigh parallel to ground, back knee nearly touches floor. Keep torso upright, chest lifted throughout. Drive through front heel to return to start. | Split squats provide stable base vs lunges, enabling heavier loads and greater muscle stimulus; Front leg performs approximately 70-80% of work while rear leg provides stability; Maintain vertical shin angle on front leg - adjust step length if knee travels excessively forward; Elevated rear heel increases quadriceps activation on front leg; Progress to Bulgarian split squats (rear foot elevated) for enhanced ROM and difficulty | McCurdy et al. (2010) J Sport Rehabil 19(1):57-70 DOI:10.1123/jsr.19.1.57; Speirs et al. (2016) J Strength Cond Res 30(2):386-392 DOI:10.1519/JSC.0000000000001096; Muyor et al. | Barbell Squat | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Erector Spinae | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Adductor Magnus, Core Stabilizers, Calves (Gastrocnemius, Soleus) | Compound | Barbell, Squat Rack | Beginner | Lower | Push | Free Weight | Position barbell on squat rack at upper chest height. Step under bar, position on upper traps/rear deltoids (high bar) or lower on rear delts (low bar). Grip bar slightly wider than shoulder-width. Unrack bar, step back, feet shoulder-width apart, toes slightly pointed out. Take deep breath, brace core, keep chest up. Initiate descent by bending hips and knees simultaneously. Lower until thighs parallel to ground or slightly below (hip crease below knee). Drive through heels to stand back up, keep knees aligned with toes throughout. | Front squats show highest vastus lateralis EMG activity, but back squats allow greater absolute loads; Muscle activation peaks at 90° knee flexion for all three quadriceps muscles; Maintain thoracic extension throughout - thoracic rounding increases injury risk and reduces force transmission; 'Spread the floor' by driving knees outward to activate hip external rotators and increase stability; Bar path should be vertical - any forward drift indicates mobility limitations or improper weight distribution | Schoenfeld (2010) J Strength Cond Res 24(12):3497-3506 DOI:10.1519/JSC.0b013e3181bac2d7; Gullett et al. (2009) J Strength Cond Res 23(1):284-292 DOI:10.1519/JSC.0b013e31818546bb; Lorenzetti et al. (2018) BMC Sports Sci Med Rehabil 10:19 DOI:10.1186/s13102-022-00404-6; Contreras et al. (2015) J Appl Biomech 31(6):452-458 DOI:10.1123/jab.2015-0091 | Powerlifting, Olympic Weightlifting, CrossFit, American Football, Rugby, Track & Field, Basketball, Soccer, Volleyball, Strongman, Bodybuilding, Skiing, Snowboarding | [TBD] | [TBD] | [TBD] |
| Barbell Step-Up | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Gluteus Medius, Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Adductor Magnus, Erector Spinae, Core Stabilizers | Compound | Barbell, Squat Rack, Box or Bench | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | Position barbell across upper back in high bar position. Stand facing box/bench that brings thigh to parallel (or slightly above) when foot on it. Place one foot entirely on elevated surface with entire sole in contact. Keep opposite foot on ground to begin. Drive through heel of elevated foot to step up onto box. Bring trailing leg up to meet working leg, standing fully on box. Step down with control, leading with same leg that stepped up. Complete all reps one side before switching, or alternate legs each rep. | Vastus lateralis and medialis show highest EMG activity during step-ups; Step-ups classified as very high glute activation exercise (>60% MVIC) but actual activation comparable to squats/lunges; Minimize push-off from trailing leg - elevated leg should perform 90%+ of work; Maintain upright torso position - excessive forward lean recruits more hamstring than quadriceps; Box height significantly affects muscle activation: higher boxes = more glute, lower boxes = more quadriceps | Muyor et al. (2020) PLOS ONE 15(4):e0230841 DOI:10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Krause Neto et al. (2020) J Sports Sci Med 19(1):91-108 PMCID:PMC7039033; Ayotte et al. | Barbell Walking Lunge | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Gluteus Medius, Adductor Magnus, Erector Spinae, Core Stabilizers, Calves | Compound | Barbell, Squat Rack | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | Position barbell across upper back in high bar position. Stand feet hip-width, core engaged. Take large step forward with one leg (2-3 feet). Lower body bending both knees until front thigh parallel to ground. Keep torso upright throughout movement. Drive through front heel, bring back leg forward to step into next lunge. Immediately step forward with opposite leg, continue walking pattern. Maintain continuous movement without pausing between reps. | Walking lunges increase balance and stabilization demands vs stationary lunges; Continuous forward momentum creates greater eccentric loading on quadriceps and glutes; Maintain consistent stride length for all reps - fatigue often causes shortened steps; Focus on vertical torso movement rather than horizontal - imagine moving between two parallel walls; This dynamic variation has superior transfer to sport-specific movement patterns | Farrokhi et al. (2008) J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 38(7):403-409 DOI:10.2519/jospt.2008.2634; Muyor et al. (2020) PLOS ONE 15(4):e0230841 DOI:10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Krause Neto et al. | Diagonal Lunge | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Adductor Magnus/Longus/Brevis, Core Stabilizers, Erector Spinae | Compound | Bodyweight | Intermediate | Lower | Push | Bodyweight | Stand feet hip-width, hands on hips or clasped in front of chest. Engage core, maintain upright posture. Step forward and at 45° angle (diagonally) with one leg. Plant front foot firmly, toes pointing forward. Lower body bending both knees until front thigh parallel to ground. Keep back knee just above floor without touching. Drive through front heel to push back to start. Alternate legs or complete all reps one side before switching. | Diagonal lunges incorporate frontal and sagittal plane movement simultaneously; 45° angle increases adductor and gluteus medius activation vs forward lunges; Maintain square hips (avoid rotation) throughout movement to maximize stability demands; Hands position affects difficulty: hands on hips (easier), arms extended forward (moderate), hands behind head (harder); Multi-planar pattern directly transfers to change-of-direction movements in sports | Muyor et al. (2020) PLOS ONE 15(4):e0230841 DOI:10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Boudreau et al. (2009) Sports Med Arthroscopy Rehabil Ther Technol 1:15 DOI:10.1186/1758-2555-1-15; Stastny et al. | Dumbbell Diagonal Lunge | Quadriceps (Vastus Lateralis, Vastus Medialis, Vastus Intermedius, Rectus Femoris), Gluteus Maximus, Gluteus Medius | Hamstrings (Biceps Femoris, Semitendinosus, Semimembranosus), Adductor Magnus/Longus/Brevis, Erector Spinae, Core Stabilizers, Forearm Flexors, Trapezius | Compound | Dumbbells | Advanced | Lower | Push | Free Weight | Hold dumbbell in each hand with arms hanging at sides. Stand feet hip-width, core engaged. Step forward and at 45° angle (diagonally) with one leg. Plant front foot firmly, toes pointing forward. Lower body bending both knees until front thigh parallel to ground. Keep dumbbells hanging at sides with shoulders back. Drive through front heel to return to start. Alternate legs or complete all reps one side before switching. | Dumbbells provide bilateral loading that challenges rotational stability more than barbell; Diagonal trajectory maximizes gluteus medius and adductor recruitment for frontal plane control; Keep dumbbells close to body throughout - avoid swinging or momentum; Maintain neutral spine and upright torso - don't allow weight to pull you into forward flexion; Progress by increasing dumbbell weight, adding pause at bottom, or performing with deficit (front foot elevated) | Muyor et al. (2020) PLOS ONE 15(4):e0230841 DOI:10.1371/journal.pone.0230841; Farrokhi et al. (2008) J Orthop Sports Phys Ther 38(7):403-409 DOI:10.2519/jospt.2008.2634; Stastny et al. ## Session 14 (Batch 7): QUADRICEPS (6), CORE (5), SHOULDERS (14) - Split Squats, Step-Ups, Lunges, Leg Extensions, Superman Variations, Shoulder Presses & Raises (25 exercises)
